 </b><p>Q. 642. Is it wrong to defer the baptism of an infant? </p> 
 <p>A. It is wrong to defer the baptism of an infant, because we thereby expose the child to the danger of dying without the Sacrament.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 643. Can we baptize a child against the wishes of its parents? </p> 
 <p>A. We cannot baptize a child against the wishes of its parents; and if the parents are not Catholics, they must not</p> 
 <p>only consent to the baptism, but also agree to bring the child up in the Catholic religion. But if a child is surely</p> 
 <p>dying, we may baptize it without either the consent or permission of its parents.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 644. How many kinds of Baptism are there? </p> 
 <p>A. There are three kinds of Baptism:</p>  
 <p>   1.Baptism of water, of desire, and of blood.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 645. What is Baptism of water? </p> 
 <p>A. Baptism of water is that which is given by pouring water on the head of the person to be baptized, and saying at the same time, "I baptize thee in the name of the Father, and of the Son, and of the Holy Ghost."</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 646. In how many ways was the baptism of water given in the first ages of the Church? </p> 
 <p>A. In the first ages of the Church, baptism of water was given in three ways, namely, by immersion or dipping, by</p> 
 <p>aspersion or sprinkling, and by infusion or pouring. Although any of these methods would be valid, only the method of infusion or pouring is now allowed in the Church.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 647. What are the chief ceremonies used in solemn baptism, and what do they signify? </p> 
 <p>A. The chief ceremonies used in solemn baptism are:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) A profession of faith and renouncement of the devil to signify our worthiness; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) The placing of salt in the mouth to signify the wisdom imparted by faith; </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) The holding of the priest's stole to signify our reception into the Church; </p> 
 <p>   4.(4) The anointing to signify the strength given by the Sacrament; </p> 
 <p>   5.(5) The giving of the white garment or cloth to signify our sinless state after baptism; and </p> 
 <p>   6.(6) The giving of the lighted candle to signify the light of faith and fire of love that should dwell in our souls.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 648. Should one who, in case of necessity, has been baptized with private baptism, be afterwards</p> 
 <p>brought to the Church to have the ceremonies of solemn baptism completed? </p> 
 <p>A. One who, in case of necessity, has been baptized with private baptism should afterwards be brought to the</p> 
 <p>Church to have the ceremonies of solemn baptism completed, because these ceremonies are commanded by the</p> 
 <p>Church and bring down blessings upon us.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 649. Is solemn baptism given with any special kind of water? </p> 
 <p>A. Solemn baptism is given with consecrated water; that is, water mixed with holy oil and blessed for baptism on</p> 
 <p>Holy Saturday and on the Saturday before Pentecost. It is always kept in the baptismal font in the baptistry -- a</p> 
 <p>place near the door of the Church set apart for baptism.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 650. What is Baptism of desire? </p> 
 <p>A. Baptism of desire is an ardent wish to receive Baptism, and to do all that God has ordained for our salvation.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 651. What is Baptism of blood? </p> 
 <p>A. Baptism of blood is the shedding of one's blood for the faith of Christ.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 652. What is the baptism of blood most commonly called? </p> 
 <p>A. The baptism of blood is most commonly called martyrdom, and those who receive it are called martyrs. It is the  death one patiently suffers from the enemies of our religion, rather than give up Catholic faith or virtue. We must  not seek martyrdom, though we must endure it when it comes.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 653. Is Baptism of desire or of blood sufficient to produce the effects of Baptism of water? </p> 
 <p>A. Baptism of desire or of blood is sufficient to produce the effects of the Baptism of water, if it is impossible to</p> 
 <p>receive the Baptism of water.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 654. How do we know that the baptism of desire or of blood will save us when it is impossible to</p> 
 <p>receive the baptism of water? </p> 
 <p>A. We know that baptism of desire or of blood will save us when it is impossible to receive the baptism of water,</p> 
 <p>from Holy Scripture, which teaches that love of God and perfect contrition can secure the remission of sins ; and</p> 
 <p>also that Our Lord promises salvation to those who lay down their life for His sake or for His teaching.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 655. What do we promise in Baptism? </p> 
 <p>A. In Baptism we promise to renounce the devil, with all his works and pomps.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 656. What do we mean by the "pomps" of the devil? </p> 
 <p>A. By the pomps of the devil we mean all worldly pride, vanities and vain shows by which people are enticed into</p> 
 <p>sin, and all foolish or sinful display of ourselves or of what we possess.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 657. Why is the name of a saint given in Baptism? </p> 
 <p>A. The name of a saint is given in Baptism in order that the person baptized may imitate his virtues and have him for a protector.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 658. What is the Saint whose name we bear called? </p> 
 <p>A. The saint whose name we bear is called our patron saint -- to whom we should have great devotion.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 659. What names should never be given in baptism? </p> 
 <p>A. These and similar names should never be given in baptism:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) The names of noted unbelievers, heretics or enemies of religion and virtue; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) The names of heathen gods, and </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) Nick-names.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 660. Why are godfathers and godmothers given in Baptism? </p> 
 <p>A. Godfathers and godmothers are given in Baptism in order that they may promise, in the name of the child, what the child itself would promise if it had the use of reason.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 661. By what other name are godfathers and godmothers called? </p> 
 <p>A. Godfathers and godmothers are usually called sponsors. Sponsors are not necessary at private baptism.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 662. Can a person ever be sponsor when absent from the baptism? </p> 
 <p>A. A person can be sponsor even when absent from the baptism, provided he has been asked and has consented</p> 
 <p>to be sponsor, and provided also some one answers the questions and touches the person to be baptized in his</p> 
 <p>name. The absent godfather or godmother is then said to be sponsor by proxy and becomes the real godparent of</p> 
 <p>the one baptized.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 663. With whom do godparents, as well as the one baptizing, contract a relationship? </p> 
 <p>A. Godparents, as well as the one baptizing, contract a spiritual relationship with the person baptized (not with his</p> 
 <p>parents), and this relationship is an impediment to marriage that must be made known to the priest in case of their</p> 
 <p>future marriage with one another. The godfather and godmother contract no relationship with each other.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 664. What questions should persons who bring a child for baptism be able to answer? </p> 
 <p>A. Persons who bring a child for baptism should be able to tell:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) The exact place where the child lives; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) The full name of its parents, and, in particular, the maiden name, or name before her marriage, of its</p> 
 <p>     mother; </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) The exact day of the month on which it was born; </p> 
 <p>   4.(4) Whether or not it has received private baptism, and </p> 
 <p>   5.(5) Whether its parents be Catholics.</p>  
 <p>Sponsors must know also the chief truths of our religion.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 665. What is the obligation of a godfather and a godmother? </p> 
 <p>A. The obligation of a godfather and a godmother is to instruct the child in its religious duties, if the parents neglect to do so or die.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 666. Can persons who are not Catholics be sponsors for Catholic children? </p> 
 <p>A. Persons who are not Catholics cannot be sponsors for Catholic children, because they cannot perform the</p> 
 <p>duties of sponsors; for if they do not know and profess the Catholic religion themselves, how can they teach it to</p> 
 <p>their godchildren? Moreover, they must answer the questions asked at baptism and declare that they believe in the</p> 
 <p>Holy Catholic Church and in all it teaches; which would be a falsehood on their part.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 667. What should parents chiefly consider in the selection of sponsors for their children? </p> 
 <p>A. In the selection of sponsors for their children parents should chiefly consider the good character and virtue of</p> 
 <p>the sponsors, selecting model Catholics to whom they would be willing at the hour of death to entrust the care and</p> 
 <p>training of their children.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 668. What dispositions must adults or grown persons, have that they may worthily receive baptism? </p> 
 <p>A. That adults may worthily receive baptism:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) They must be willing to receive it; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) They must have faith in Christ; </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) They must have true sorrow for their sins, and </p> 
 <p>   4.(4) They must solemnly renounce the devil and all his works; that is, all sin.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 669. What is the ceremony of churching? </p> 
 <p>A. The ceremony of churching is a particular blessing which a mother receives at the Altar, as soon as she is able</p> 
 <p>to present herself in the Church after the birth of her child. In this ceremony the priest invokes God's blessing on</p> 
 <p>the mother and child, while she on her part returns thanks to God</p> 
 <p>.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>LESSON FIFTEENTH: On Confirmation </p>  <b>
 </b><p>Q. 670. What is Confirmation? </p> 
 <p>A. Confirmation is a Sacrament through which we receive the Holy Ghost to make us strong and perfect Christians and soldiers of Jesus Christ.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 671. When was Confirmation instituted? </p> 
 <p>A. The exact time at which Confirmation was instituted is not known. But as this Sacrament was administered by</p> 
 <p>the Apostles and numbered with the other Sacraments instituted by Our Lord, it is certain that He instituted this</p> 
 <p>Sacrament also and instructed His Apostles in its use, at some time before His ascension into heaven.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 672. Why is Confirmation so called? </p> 
 <p>A. Confirmation is so called from its chief effect, which is to strengthen or render us more firm in whatever belongs to our faith and religious duties.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 673. Why are we called soldiers of Jesus Christ? </p> 
 <p>A. We are called soldiers of Jesus Christ to indicate how we must resist the attacks of our spiritual enemies and</p> 
 <p>secure our victory over them by following and obeying Our Lord.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 674. May one add a new name to his own at Confirmation? </p> 
 <p>A. One may and should add a new name to his own at Confirmation, especially when the name of a saint has not</p> 
 <p>been given in Baptism.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 675. Who administers Confirmation? </p> 
 <p>A. The bishop is the ordinary minister of Confirmation.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 676. Why do we say the bishop is the "ordinary minister" of Confirmation? </p> 
 <p>A. We say the bishop is the ordinary minister of Confirmation because in some foreign missions, where bishops</p> 
 <p>have not yet been appointed, the Holy Father permits one of the priests to administer Confirmation with the Holy</p> 
 <p>Oil blessed by the bishop.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 677. How does the bishop give Confirmation? </p> 
 <p>A. The bishop extends his hands over those who are to be confirmed, prays that they may receive the Holy Ghost,</p> 
 <p>and anoints the forehead of each with holy chrism in the form of a cross.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 678. In Confirmation, what does the extending of the bishop's hands over us signify? </p> 
 <p>A. In Confirmation, the extending of the bishop's hands over us signifies the descent of the Holy Ghost upon us and the special protection of God through the grace of Confirmation.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 679. What is holy chrism? </p> 
 <p>A. Holy chrism is a mixture of olive-oil and balm, consecrated by the bishop.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 680. What do the oil and balm in Holy Chrism signify? </p> 
 <p>A. In Holy Chrism, the oil signifies strength, and the balm signifies the freedom from corruption and the sweetness which virtue must give to our lives.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 681. How many holy oils are used in the Church? </p> 
 <p>A. Three holy oils are used in the Church, namely, the oil of the sick, the oil of catechumens, and holy chrism.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 682. What constitutes the difference between these oils? </p> 
 <p>A. The form of prayer or blessing alone constitutes the difference between these oils; for they are all olive oil, but in the Holy Chrism, balm is mixed with the oil.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 683. When and by whom are the holy oils blessed? </p> 
 <p>A. The holy oils are blessed at the Mass on Holy Thursday by the bishop, who alone has the right to bless them.</p> 
 <p>After the blessing they are distributed to the priests of the diocese, who must then burn what remains of the old oils and use the newly blessed oils for the coming year.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 684. For what are the holy oils used? </p> 
 <p>A. The holy oils are used as follows: The oil of the sick is used for Extreme Unction and for some blessings; the oil of catechumens is used for Baptism and Holy Orders. Holy Chrism is used at Baptism and for the blessing of some sacred things, such as altars, chalices, church-bells, etc., which are usually blessed by a bishop.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 685. What does the bishop say in anointing the person he confirms? </p> 
 <p>A. In anointing the person he confirms the bishop says: "I sign thee with the sign of the cross, and I confirm thee</p> 
 <p>with the chrism of salvation, in the name of the Father, and of the Son, and of the Holy Ghost."</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 686. What is meant by anointing the forehead with chrism in the form of a cross? </p> 
 <p>A. By anointing the forehead with chrism in the form of a cross is meant that the Christian who is confirmed must</p> 
 <p>openly profess and practice his faith, never be ashamed of it; and rather die than deny it.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 687. When must we openly profess and practice our religion? </p> 
 <p>A. We must openly profess and practice our religion as often as we cannot do otherwise without violating some</p> 
 <p>law of God or of His Church.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 688. Why have we good reason never to be ashamed of the Catholic faith? </p> 
 <p>A. We have good reason never to be ashamed of the Catholic Faith because it is the Old Faith established by</p> 
 <p>Christ and taught by His Apostles; it is the Faith for which countless Holy Martyrs suffered and died; it is the Faith that has brought true civilization, with all its benefits, into the world, and it is the only Faith that can truly reform and preserve public and private morals.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 689. Why does the bishop give the person he confirms a slight blow on the cheek? </p> 
 <p>A. The bishop gives the person he confirms a slight blow on the cheek, to put him in mind that he must be ready to suffer everything, even death, for the sake of Christ.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 690. Is it right to test ourselves through our imagination of what we would be willing to suffer for the</p> 
 <p>sake of Christ? </p> 
 <p>A. It is not right to test ourselves through our imagination of what we would be willing to suffer for the sake of</p> 
 <p>Christ, for such tests may lead us into sin. When a real test comes we are assured God will give to us, as He did to</p> 
 <p>the Holy Martyrs, sufficient grace to endure it.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 691. To receive Confirmation worthily is it necessary to be in the state of grace? </p> 
 <p>A. To receive Confirmation worthily it is necessary to be in the state of grace.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 692. What special preparation should be made to receive Confirmation? </p> 
 <p>A. Persons of an age to learn should know the chief mysteries of faith and the duties of a Christian, and be</p> 
 <p>instructed in the nature and effects of this Sacrament.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 693. Why should we know the chief mysteries of faith and the duties of a Christian before receiving</p> 
 <p>Confirmation? </p> 
 <p>A. We should know the Chief Mysteries of Faith and the duties of a Christian before receiving Confirmation</p> 
 <p>because as one cannot be a good soldier without knowing the rules of the army to which he belongs and</p> 
 <p>understanding the commands of his leader, so one cannot be a good Christian without knowing the laws of the</p> 
 <p>Church and understanding the commands of Christ.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 694. Is it a sin to neglect Confirmation? </p> 
 <p>A. It is a sin to neglect Confirmation, especially in these evil days when faith and morals are exposed to so many</p> 
 <p>and such violent temptations.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 695. What do we mean by "these evil days"? </p> 
 <p>A. By "these evil days" we mean the present age or century in which we are living, surrounded on all sides by</p> 
 <p>unbelief, false doctrines, bad books, bad example and temptation in every form.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 696. Is Confirmation necessary for salvation? </p> 
 <p>A. Confirmation is not so necessary for salvation that we could not be saved without it, for it is not given to infants even in danger of death; nevertheless, there is a divine command obliging all to receive it, if possible. Persons who have not been confirmed in youth should make every effort to be confirmed later in life.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 697. Are sponsors necessary in Confirmation? </p> 
 <p>A. Sponsors are necessary in Confirmation, and they must be of the same good character as those required at</p> 
 <p>Baptism, for they take upon themselves the same duties and responsibilities. They also contract a spiritual</p> 
 <p>relationship, which, however, unlike that in Baptism, is not an impediment to marriage.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 698. Which are the effects of Confirmation? </p> 
 <p>A. The effects of Confirmation are an increase of sanctifying grace, the strengthening of our faith, and the gifts of</p> 
 <p>the Holy Ghost.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 699. Which are the gifts of the Holy Ghost? </p> 
 <p>A. The gifts of the Holy Ghost are Wisdom, Understanding, Counsel, Fortitude, Knowledge, Piety, and Fear of</p> 
 <p>the Lord.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 700. Why do we receive the gift of Fear of the Lord? </p> 
 <p>A. We receive the gift of Fear of the Lord to fill us with a dread of sin.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 701. Why do we receive the gift of Piety? </p> 
 <p>A. We receive the gift of Piety to make us love God as a Father, and obey Him because we love Him.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 702. Why do we receive the gift of Knowledge? </p> 
 <p>A. We receive the gift of Knowledge to enable us to discover the will of God in all things.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 703. Why do we receive the gift of Fortitude? </p> 
 <p>A. We receive the gift of Fortitude to strengthen us to do the will of God in all things.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 704. Why do we receive the gift of Counsel? </p> 
 <p>A. We receive the gift of Counsel to warn us of the deceits of the devil, and of the dangers to salvation.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 705. How is it clear that the devil could easily deceive us if the Holy Ghost did not aid us? </p> 
 <p>A. It is clear that the devil could easily deceive us if the Holy Ghost did not aid us, for just as our sins do not</p> 
 <p>deprive us of our knowledge, so the devil's sin did not deprive him of the great intelligence and power which he</p> 
 <p>possessed as an angel. Moreover, his experience in the world extends over all ages and places, while ours is</p> 
 <p>confined to a few years and to a limited number of places.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 706. Why do we receive the gift of Understanding? </p> 
 <p>A. We receive the gift of Understanding to enable us to know more clearly the mysteries of faith.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 707. Why do we receive the gift of Wisdom? </p> 
 <p>A. We receive the gift of Wisdom to give us a relish for the things of God, and to direct our whole life and all our</p> 
 <p>actions to His honor and glory.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 708. Which are the Beatitudes? </p> 
 <p>A. The Beatitudes are:</p>  
 <p>   1.1. Blessed are the poor in spirit, for theirs is the kingdom of heaven. </p> 
 <p>   2.2. Blessed are the meek, for they shall possess the land. </p> 
 <p>   3.3. Blessed are they that mourn, for they shall be comforted. </p> 
 <p>   4.4. Blessed are they that hunger and thirst after justice, for they shall be filled. </p> 
 <p>   5.5. Blessed are the merciful, for they shall obtain mercy. </p> 
 <p>   6.6. Blessed are the clean of heart, for they shall see God. </p> 
 <p>   7.7. Blessed are the peacemakers, for they shall be called the children of God. </p> 
 <p>   8.8. Blessed are they that suffer persecution for justice' sake, for theirs is the kingdom of heaven.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 709. What are the Beatitudes and why are they so called? </p> 
 <p>A. The Beatitudes are a portion of Our Lord's Sermon on the Mount, and they are so called because each of them</p> 
 <p>holds out a promised reward to those who practice the virtues they recommend.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 710. Where did Our Lord usually preach? </p> 
 <p>A. Our Lord usually preached wherever an opportunity of doing good by His Words presented itself. He preached</p> 
 <p>at times in the synagogues or meeting-houses but more frequently in the open air -- by the seashore or on the</p> 
 <p>mountain, and often by the wayside.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 711. What is the meaning and use of the Beatitudes in general? </p> 
 <p>A. </p>  
 <p>   1.(1) In general the Beatitudes embrace whatever pertains to the perfection of Christian life, and they invite us</p> 
 <p>     to the practice of the highest Christian virtues. </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) In different forms they all promise the same reward, namely, sanctifying grace in this life and eternal glory</p> 
 <p>     in the next. </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) They offer us encouragement and consolation for every trial and affliction.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 712. What does the first Beatitude mean by the "poor in spirit"? </p> 
 <p>A. The first Beatitude means by the "poor in spirit" all persons, rich or poor, who would not offend God to possess or retain anything that this world can give; and who, when necessity or charity requires it, give willingly for the glory of God. It includes also those who humbly submit to their condition in life when it cannot be improved by lawful means.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 713. Who are the mourners who deserve the consolation promised in the third Beatitude? </p> 
 <p>A. The mourners who deserve the consolation promised in the third Beatitude are they who, out of love for God,</p> 
 <p>bewail their own sins and those of the world; and they who patiently endure all trials that come from God or for His sake.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 714. What lessons do the other Beatitudes convey? </p> 
 <p>A. The other Beatitudes convey these lessons: The meek suppress all feelings of anger and humbly submit to</p> 
 <p>whatever befalls them by the Will of God; and they never desire to do evil for evil. The justice after which we</p> 
 <p>should seek is every Christian virtue included under that name, and we are told that if we earnestly desire and seek it we shall obtain it. The persecuted for justice' sake are they who will not abandon their faith or virtue for any cause.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 715. Who may be rightly called merciful? </p> 
 <p>A. The merciful are they who practice the corporal and spiritual works of mercy, and who aid by word or deed</p> 
 <p>those who need their help for soul or body.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 716. Why are the clean of heart promised so great a reward? </p> 
 <p>A. The clean of heart, that is, the truly virtuous, whose thoughts, desires, words and works are pure and modest,</p> 
 <p>are promised so great a reward because the chaste and sinless have always been the most intimate friends of God.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 717. What is the duty of a peacemaker? </p> 
 <p>A. It is the duty of a peacemaker to avoid and prevent quarrels, reconcile enemies, and to put an end to all evil</p> 
 <p>reports of others or evil speaking against them. As peacemakers are called the children of God, disturbers of</p> 
 <p>peace should be called the children of the devil.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 718. Why does Our Lord speak in particular of poverty, meekness, sorrow, desire for virtue, mercy,</p> 
 <p>purity, peace and suffering? </p> 
 <p>A. Our Lord speaks in particular of poverty, meekness, sorrow, desire for virtue, mercy, purity, peace and</p> 
 <p>suffering because these are the chief features in His own earthly life; poverty in His birth, life and death; meekness in His teaching; sorrow at all times. He eagerly sought to do good, showed mercy to all, recommended chastity, brought peace, and patiently endured suffering.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 719. Which are the twelve fruits of the Holy Ghost? </p> 
 <p>A. The twelve fruits of the Holy Ghost are Charity, Joy, Peace, Patience, Benignity, Goodness, Long-suffering,</p> 
 <p>Mildness, Faith, Modesty, Continency, and Chastity.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 720. Why are charity, joy, peace, etc., called fruits of the Holy Ghost? </p> 
 <p>A. Charity, joy, peace, etc., are called fruits of the Holy Ghost because they grow in our souls out of the seven</p> 
 <p>gifts of the Holy Ghost.</p>  
 <p> </p> 
 <p> </p> 
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>LESSON SEVENTEENTH: On the Sacrament of Penance </p>  <b>
 </b><p>Q. 721. What is the Sacrament of Penance? </p> 
 <p>A. Penance is a Sacrament in which the sins committed after Baptism are forgiven.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q 722. Has the word Penance any other meaning? </p> 
 <p>A. The word Penance has other meanings. It means also those punishments we inflict upon ourselves as a means of atoning for our past sins; it means likewise that disposition of the heart in which we detest and bewail our sins</p> 
 <p>because they were offensive to God.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 723. How does the institution of the Sacrament of Penance show the goodness of Our Lord? </p> 
 <p>A. The institution of the Sacrament of Penance shows the goodness of Our Lord, because having once saved us</p> 
 <p>through Baptism, He might have left us to perish if we again committed sin.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 724. What are the natural benefits of the Sacrament of Penance? </p> 
 <p>A. The natural benefits of the Sacrament of Penance are: It gives us in our confessor a true friend, to whom we can go in all our trials and to whom we can confide our secrets with the hope of obtaining advice and relief.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 725. How does the Sacrament of Penance remit sin, and restore to the soul the friendship of God? </p> 
 <p>A. The Sacrament of Penance remits sin and restores the friendship of God to the soul by means of the absolution</p> 
 <p>of the priest.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 726. What is Absolution? </p> 
 <p>A. Absolution is the form of prayer or words the priest pronounces over us with uplifted hand when he forgives the sins we have confessed. It is given while we are saying the Act of Contrition after receiving our Penance.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 727. Does the priest ever refuse absolution to a penitent? </p> 
 <p>A. The priest must and does refuse absolution to a penitent when he thinks the penitent is not rightly disposed for</p> 
 <p>the Sacrament. He sometimes postpones the absolution till the next confession, either for the good of the penitent</p> 
 <p>or for the sake of better preparation -- especially when the person has been a long time from confession.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 728. What should a person do when the priest has refused or postponed absolution? </p> 
 <p>A. When the priest has refused or postponed absolution, the penitent should humbly submit to his decision, follow</p> 
 <p>his instructions, and endeavor to remove whatever prevented the giving of the absolution and return to the same</p> 
 <p>confessor with the necessary dispositions and resolution of amendment.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 729. Can the priest forgive all sins in the Sacrament of Penance? </p> 
 <p>A. The priest has the power to forgive all sins in the Sacrament of Penance, but he may not have the authority to</p> 
 <p>forgive all. To forgive sins validly in the Sacrament of Penance, two things are required:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) The power to forgive sins which every priest receives at his ordination, and </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) The right to use that power which must be given by the bishop, who authorizes the priest to hear</p> 
 <p>     confessions and pass judgment on the sins.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 730. What are the sins called which the priest has no authority to absolve? </p> 
 <p>A. The sins which the priest has no authority to absolve are called reserved sins. Absolution from these sins can be obtained only from the bishop, and sometimes only from the Pope, or by his special permission. Persons having a reserved sin to confess cannot be absolved from any of their sins till the priest receives faculties or authority to absolve the reserved sin also.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 731. Why is the absolution from some sins reserved to the Pope or bishop? </p> 
 <p>A. The absolution from some sins is reserved to the Pope or bishop to deter or prevent, by this special restriction,</p> 
 <p>persons from committing them, either on account of the greatness of the sin itself or on account of its evil</p> 
 <p>consequences.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 732. Can any priest absolve a person in danger of death from reserved sins without the permission of</p> 
 <p>the bishop? </p> 
 <p>A. Any priest can absolve a person in danger of death from reserved sins without the permission of the bishop,</p> 
 <p>because at the hour of death the Church removes these restrictions in order to save, if possible, the soul of the</p> 
 <p>dying.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 733. How do you know that the priest has the power of absolving from the sins committed after</p> 
 <p>Baptism? </p> 
 <p>A. I know that the priest has the power of absolving from the sins committed after Baptism, because Jesus Christ</p> 
 <p>granted that power to the priests of His Church when He said: "Receive ye the Holy Ghost. Whose sins you shall</p> 
 <p>forgive, they are forgiven them; whose sins you shall retain, they are retained."</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 734. How do we know that Our Lord, while on earth, had the power to forgive sins? </p> 
 <p>A. We know that Our Lord, while on earth, had the power to forgive sins:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) Because He was always God, and; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) Because He frequently did forgive sins and proved their forgiveness by miracles.</p>  
 <p>Since He had the power Himself, He could give it to His Apostles.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 735. Was the power to forgive sins given to the apostles alone? </p> 
 <p>A. The power to forgive sins was not given to the apostles alone, because it was not given for the benefit merely of those who lived at the time of the apostles, but for all who, having grievously sinned, after Baptism, should need forgiveness. Since, therefore, Baptism will be given till the end of time, and since the danger of sinning after it always remains the power to absolve from such sins must also remain in the Church till the end of time.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 736. When was the Sacrament of Penance instituted? </p> 
 <p>A. The Sacrament of Penance was instituted after the resurrection of Our Lord, when He gave to His apostles the</p> 
 <p>power to forgive sins, which He had promised to them before His death.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 737. Are the enemies of our religion right when they say man cannot forgive sins? </p> 
 <p>A. The enemies of our religion are right when they say man cannot forgive sins if they mean that he cannot forgive them by his own power, but they are certainly wrong if they mean that he cannot forgive them even by the power of God, for man can do anything if God gives him the power. The priest does not forgive sins by his own power as man, but by the authority he receives as the minister of God.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 738. How do the priests of the Church exercise the power of forgiving sins? </p> 
 <p>A. The priests of the Church exercise the power of forgiving sins by hearing the confession of sins, and granting</p> 
 <p>pardon for them as ministers of God and in His name.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 739. How does the power to forgive sins imply the obligation of going to confession? </p> 
 <p>A. The power to forgive sins implies the obligation of going to confession because as sins are usually committed</p> 
 <p>secretly, the priest could never know what sins to forgive and what not to forgive, unless the sins committed were</p> 
 <p>made known to him by the persons guilty of them.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 740. Could God not forgive our sins if we confessed them to Himself in secret? </p> 
 <p>A. Certainly, God could forgive our sins if we confessed them to Himself in secret, but He has not promised to do</p> 
 <p>so; whereas He has promised to pardon them if we confess them to His priests. Since He is free to pardon or not</p> 
 <p>to pardon, He has the right to establish a Sacrament through which alone He will pardon.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 741. What must we do to receive the Sacrament of Penance worthily? </p> 
 <p>A. To receive the Sacrament of Penance worthily we must do five things:</p>  
 <p>   1.1. We must examine our conscience. </p> 
 <p>   2.2. We must have sorrow for our sins. </p> 
 <p>   3.3. We must make a firm resolution never more to offend God. </p> 
 <p>   4.4. We must confess our sins to the priest. </p> 
 <p>   5.5. We must accept the penance which the priest gives us.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 742. What should we pray for in preparing for confession? </p> 
 <p>A. In preparing for confession we should pray to the Holy Ghost to give us light to know our sins and to</p> 
 <p>understand their guilt; for grace to detest them; for courage to confess them and for strength to keep our</p> 
 <p>resolutions.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 743. What faults do many commit in preparing for confession? </p> 
 <p>A. In preparing for confession many commit the faults:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) Of giving too much time to the examination of conscience and little or none in exciting themselves to true</p> 
 <p>     sorrow for the sins discovered; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) Of trying to recall every trifling circumstance, instead of thinking of the means by which they will avoid</p> 
 <p>     their sins for the future.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 744. What, then, is the most important part of the preparation for confession? </p> 
 <p>A. The most important part of the preparation for confession is sincere sorrow for the sins committed and the firm</p> 
 <p>determination to avoid them for the future.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 745. What is the chief reason that our confessions do not always amend our way of living? </p> 
 <p>A. The chief reason that our confessions do not always amend our way of living is our want of real earnest</p> 
 <p>preparation for them and the fact that we have not truly convinced ourselves of the need of amendment. We often</p> 
 <p>confess our sins more from habit, necessity or fear than from a real desire of receiving grace and of being restored</p> 
 <p>to the friendship of God.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 746. What faults are to be avoided in making our confession? </p> 
 <p>A. In making our confession we are to avoid:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) Telling useless details, the sins of others, or the name of any person; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) Confessing sins we are not sure of having committed; exaggerating our sins or their number; multiplying</p> 
 <p>     the number of times a day by the number of days to get the exact number of habitual sins; </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) Giving a vague answer, such as "sometimes," when asked how often; waiting after each sin to be asked</p> 
 <p>     for the next; </p> 
 <p>   4.(4) Hesitating over sins through pretented modesty and thus delaying the priests and others; telling the exact</p> 
 <p>     words in each when we have committed several sins of the same kind, cursing, for example; and, lastly,</p> 
 <p>     leaving the confessional before the priest gives us a sign to go.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 747. Is it wrong to go to confession out of your turn against the will of others waiting with you? </p> 
 <p>A. It is wrong to go to confession out of our turn against the will of others waiting with us, because:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) It causes disorder, quarreling and scandalous conduct in the Church; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) It is unjust, makes others angry and lessens their good dispositions for confession; </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) It annoys and distracts the priest by the confusion and disorder it creates.</p>  
 <p>It is better to wait than go to confession in an excited and disorderly manner.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 748. What should a penitent do who knows he cannot perform the penance given? </p> 
 <p>A. A penitent who knows he cannot perform the penance given should ask the priest for one that he can perform.</p> 
 <p>When we forget the penance given we must ask for it again, for we cannot fulfill our duty by giving ourselves a</p> 
 <p>penance. The penance must be performed at the time and in the manner the confessor directs.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 749. What is the examination of conscience? </p> 
 <p>A. The examination of conscience is an earnest effort to recall to mind all the sins we have committed since our last worthy confession.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 750. When is our confession worthy? </p> 
 <p>A. Our confession is worthy when we have done all that is required for a good confession, and when, through the</p> 
 <p>absolution, our sins are really forgiven.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 751. How can we make a good examination of conscience? </p> 
 <p>A. We can make a good examination of conscience by calling to memory the commandments of God, the precepts of the Church, the seven capital sins, and the particular duties of our state in life, to find out the sins we have committed.</p>  <b>
 </b><p>Q. 752. What should we do before beginning the examination of conscience? </p> 
 <p>A. Before beginning the examination of conscience we should pray to God to give us light to know our sins and</p> 
 <p>grace to detest them.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>LESSON EIGHTEENTH: On Contrition </p>  <b>
 </b><p>Q. 753. What is contrition, or sorrow for sin? </p> 
 <p>A. Contrition, or sorrow for sin, is a hatred of sin and a true grief of the soul for having offended God, with a firm</p> 
 <p>purpose of sinning no more.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 754. Give an example of how we should hate and avoid sin. </p> 
 <p>A. We should hate and avoid sin as one hates and avoids a poison that almost caused his death. We may not</p> 
 <p>grieve over the death of our soul as we do over the death of a friend, and yet our sorrow may be true; because the</p> 
 <p>sorrow for sin comes more from our reason than from our feelings.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 755. What kind of sorrow should we have for our sins? </p> 
 <p>A. The sorrow we should have for our sins should be interior, supernatural, universal, and sovereign.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 756. What do you mean by saying that our sorrow should be interior? </p> 
 <p>A. When I say that our sorrow should be interior, I mean that it should come from the heart, and not merely from</p> 
 <p>the lips.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 757. What do you mean by saying that our sorrow should be supernatural? </p> 
 <p>A. When I say that our sorrow should be supernatural, I mean that it should be prompted by the grace of God,</p> 
 <p>and excited by motives which spring from faith, and not by merely natural motives.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 758. What do we mean by "motives that spring from faith" and by "merely natural motives" with</p> 
 <p>regard to sorrow for sin? </p> 
 <p>A. By sorrow for sin from "motives that spring from faith," we mean sorrow for reasons that God has made known to us, such as the loss of heaven, the fear of hell or purgatory, or the dread of afflictions that come from God in punishment for sin. By "merely natural motives" we mean sorrow for reasons made known to us by our own experience or by the experience of others, such as loss of character, goods or health. A motive is whatever moves our will to do or avoid anything.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 759. What do you mean by saying that our sorrow should be universal? </p> 
 <p>A. When I say that our sorrow should be universal, I mean that we should be sorry for all our mortal sins without</p> 
 <p>exception.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 760. Why cannot some of our mortal sins be forgiven while the rest remain on our souls? </p> 
 <p>A. It is impossible for any of our mortal sins to be forgiven unless they are all forgiven, because as light and</p> 
 <p>darkness cannot be together in the same place, so sanctifying grace and mortal sin cannot dwell together. If there</p> 
 <p>be grace in the soul, there can be no mortal sin, and if there be mortal sin, there can be no grace, for one mortal sin expels all grace.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 761. What do you mean when you say that our sorrow should be sovereign? </p> 
 <p>A. When I say that our sorrow should be sovereign, I mean that we should grieve more for having offended God</p> 
 <p>than for any other evil that can befall us.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 762. Why should we be sorry for our sins? </p> 
 <p>A. We should be sorry for our sins because sin is the greatest of evils and an offense against God our Creator,</p> 
 <p>Preserver, and Redeemer, and because it shuts us out of heaven and condemns us to the eternal pains of hell.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 763. How do we show that sin is the greatest of all evils? </p> 
 <p>A. We show that sin is the greatest of evils because its effects last the longest and have the most terrible</p> 
 <p>consequences. All the misfortunes of this world can last only for a time, and we escape them at death, whereas the</p> 
 <p>evils caused by sin keep with us for all eternity and are only increased at death.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 764. How many kinds of contrition are there? </p> 
 <p>A. There are two kinds of contrition; perfect contrition and imperfect contrition.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 765. What is perfect contrition? </p> 
 <p>A. Perfect contrition is that which fills us with sorrow and hatred for sin, because it offends God, who is infinitely</p> 
 <p>good in Himself and worthy of all love.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 766. When will perfect contrition obtain pardon for mortal sin without the Sacrament of Penance? </p> 
 <p>A. Perfect contrition will obtain pardon for mortal sin without the Sacrament of Penance when we cannot go to</p> 
 <p>confession, but with the perfect contrition we must have the intention of going to confession as soon as possible, if we again have the opportunity.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 767. What is imperfect contrition? </p> 
 <p>A. Imperfect contrition is that by which we hate what offends God because by it we lose heaven and deserve hell;</p> 
 <p>or because sin is so hateful in itself.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 768. What other name is given to imperfect contrition and why is it called imperfect? </p> 
 <p>A. Imperfect contrition is called attrition. It is called imperfect only because it is less perfect than the highest grade of contrition by which we are sorry for sin out of pure love of God's own goodness and without any consideration  of what befalls ourselves.</p> <b> 
 </b><p> </p> 
 <p>Q. 769. Is imperfect contrition sufficient for a worthy confession? </p> 
 <p>A. Imperfect contrition is sufficient for a worthy confession, but we should endeavor to have perfect contrition.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 770. What do you mean by a firm purpose of sinning no more? </p> 
 <p>A. By a firm purpose of sinning no more I mean a fixed resolve not only to avoid all mortal sin, but also its near</p> 
 <p>occasions.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 771. What do you mean by the near occasions of sin? </p> 
 <p>A. By the near occasions of sin I mean all the persons, places and things that may easily lead us into sin.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 772. Why are we bound to avoid occasions of sin? </p> 
 <p>A. We are bound to avoid occasions of sin because Our Lord has said: "He who loves the danger will perish in it"; and as we are bound to avoid the loss of our souls, so we are bound to avoid the danger of their loss. The</p> 
 <p>occasion is the cause of sin, and you cannot take away the evil without removing its cause.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 773. Is a person who is determined to avoid the sin, but who is unwilling to give up its near occasion</p> 
 <p>when it is possible to do so, rightly disposed for confession? </p> 
 <p>A. A person who is determined to avoid the sin, but who is unwilling to give up its near occasion when it is possible to do so, is not rightly disposed for confession, and he will not be absolved if he makes known to the priest the true state of his conscience.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 774. How many kinds of occasions of sin are there? </p> 
 <p>A. There are four kinds of occasions of sin:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) Near occasions, through which we always fall; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) Remote occasions, through which we sometimes fall; </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) Voluntary occasions or those we can avoid; and </p> 
 <p>   4.(4) Involuntary occasions or those we cannot avoid.</p>  
 <p>A person who lives in a near and voluntary occasion of sin need not expect forgiveness while he continues in that</p> 
 <p>state.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 775. What persons, places and things are usually occasions of sin? </p> 
 <p>A. </p>  
 <p>   1.(1) The persons who are occasions of sin are all those in whose company we sin, whether they be bad of</p> 
 <p>     themselves or bad only while in our company, in which case we also become occasions of sin for them; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) The places are usually liquor saloons, low theaters, indecent dances, entertainments, amusements,</p> 
 <p>     exhibitions, and all immoral resorts of any kind, whether we sin in them or not; </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) The things are all bad books, indecent pictures, songs, jokes and the like, even when they are tolerated</p> 
 <p>     by public opinion and found in public places.</p>  
 <p> </p> 
 <p> </p> 
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>LESSON NINETEENTH: On Confession </p>  <b>
 </b><p>Q. 776. What is Confession? </p> 
 <p>A. Confession is the telling of our sins to a duly authorized priest, for the purpose of obtaining forgiveness.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 777. Who is a duly authorized priest? </p> 
 <p>A. A duly authorized priest is one sent to hear confessions by the lawful bishop of the diocese in which we are at</p> 
 <p>the time of our confession.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 778. Is it ever allowed to write our sins and read them to the priest in the confessional or give them to</p> 
 <p>him to read? </p> 
 <p>A. It is allowed, when necessary, to write our sins and read them to the priest, as persons do who have almost</p> 
 <p>entirely lost their memory. It is also allowed to give the paper to the priest, as persons do who have lost the use of</p> 
 <p>their speech. In such cases the paper must, after the confession, be carefully destroyed either by the priest or the</p> 
 <p>penitent.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 779. What is to be done when persons must make their confession and cannot find a priest who</p> 
 <p>understands their language? </p> 
 <p>A. Persons who must make their confession and who cannot find a priest who understands their language, must</p> 
 <p>confess as best they can by some signs, showing what sins they wish to confess and how they are sorry for them.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 780. What sins are we bound to confess? </p> 
 <p>A. We are bound to confess all our mortal sins, but it is well also to confess our venial sins.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 781. Why is it well to confess also the venial sins we remember? </p> 
 <p>A. It is well to confess also the venial sins we remember:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) Because it shows our hatred of all sin, and </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) Because it is sometimes difficult to determine just when a sin is venial and when mortal.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 782. What should one do who has only venial sins to confess? </p> 
 <p>A. One who has only venial sins to confess should tell also some sin already confessed in his past life for which he knows he is truly sorry; because it is not easy to be truly sorry for slight sins and imperfections, and yet we must be sorry for the sins confessed that our confession may be valid -- hence we add some past sin for which we are truly sorry to those for which we may not be sufficiently sorry.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 783. Should a person stay from confession because he thinks he has no sin to confess ? </p> 
 <p>A. A person should not stay from confession because he thinks he has no sin to confess, for the Sacrament of</p> 
 <p>Penance, besides forgiving sin, gives an increase of sanctifying grace, and of this we have always need, especially</p> 
 <p>to resist temptation. The Saints, who were almost without imperfection, went to confession frequently.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 784. Should a person go to Communion after confession even when the confessor does not bid him go?</p> 
 <p>A. A person should go to Communion after confession even when the confessor does not bid him go, because the</p> 
 <p>confessor so intends unless he positively forbids his penitent to receive Communion. However, one who has not</p> 
 <p>yet received his first Communion should not go to Communion after confession, even if the confessor by mistake</p> 
 <p>should bid him go.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 785. Which are the chief qualities of a good Confession? </p> 
 <p>A. The chief qualities of a good Confession are three: it must be humble, sincere, and entire.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 786. When is our Confession humble? </p> 
 <p>A. Our Confession is humble when we accuse ourselves of our sins, with a deep sense of shame and sorrow for</p> 
 <p>having offended God.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 787. When is our Confession sincere? </p> 
 <p>A. Our Confession is sincere when we tell our sins honestly and truthfully, neither exaggerating nor excusing them.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 788. Why is it wrong to accuse ourselves of sins we have not committed? </p> 
 <p>A. It is wrong to accuse ourselves of sins we have not committed, because, by our so doing, the priest cannot</p> 
 <p>know the true state of our souls, as he must do before giving us absolution.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 789. When is our Confession entire? </p> 
 <p>A. Our Confession is entire when we tell the number and kinds of our sins and the circumstances which change</p> 
 <p>their nature.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 790. What do you mean by the "kinds of sin?" </p> 
 <p>A. By the "kinds of sin," we mean the particular division or class to which the sins belong; that is, whether they be sins of blasphemy, disobedience, anger, impurity, dishonesty, etc. We can determine the kind of sin by discovering the commandment or precept of the Church we have broken or the virtue against which we have acted.</p>  <b>
 </b><p>Q. 791. What do we mean by "circumstances which change the nature of sins?" </p> 
 <p>A. By "circumstances which change the nature of sins" we mean anything that makes it another kind of sin. Thus to steal is a sin, but to steal from the Church makes our theft sacrilegious. Again, impure actions are sins, but a person  must say whether they were committed alone or with others, with relatives or strangers, with persons married or single, etc., because these circumstances change them from one kind of impurity to another.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 792. What should we do if we cannot remember the number of our sins? </p> 
 <p>A. If we cannot remember the number of our sins, we should tell the number as nearly as possible, and say how</p> 
 <p>often we may have sinned in a day, a week, or a month, and how long the habit or practice has lasted.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 793. Is our Confession worthy if, without our fault, we forget to confess a mortal sin? </p> 
 <p>A. If without our fault we forget to confess a mortal sin, our Confession is worthy, and the sin is forgiven; but it</p> 
 <p>must be told in Confession if it again comes to our mind.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 794. May a person who has forgotten to tell a mortal sin in confession go to Holy Communion before</p> 
 <p>going again to confession? </p> 
 <p>A. A person who has forgotten to tell a mortal sin in confession may go to communion before again going to</p> 
 <p>confession, because the forgotten sin was forgiven with those confessed, and the confession was good and worthy.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 795. Is it a grievous offense willfully to conceal a mortal sin in Confession? </p> 
 <p>A. It is a grievous offense willfully to conceal a mortal sin in Confession, because we thereby tell a lie to the Holy</p> 
 <p>Ghost, and make our Confession worthless.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 796. How is concealing a sin telling a lie to the Holy Ghost? </p> 
 <p>A. Concealing a sin is telling a lie to the Holy Ghost, because he who conceals the sin declares in confession to</p> 
 <p>God and the priest that he committed no sins but what he has confessed, while the Holy Ghost, the Spirit of Truth,</p> 
 <p>saw him committing the sin he now conceals and still sees it in his soul while he denies it.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 797. Why is it foolish to conceal sins in confession? </p> 
 <p>A. It is foolish to conceal sins in confession:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) Because we thereby make our spiritual condition worse; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) We must tell the sin sometime if we ever hope to be saved; </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) It will be made known on the day of judgment, before the world, whether we conceal it now or confess</p> 
 <p>     it.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 798. What must he do who has willfully concealed a mortal sin in Confession? </p> 
 <p>A. He who has willfully concealed a mortal sin in Confession must not only confess it, but must also repeat all the</p> 
 <p>sins he has committed since his last worthy Confession.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 799. Must one who has willfully concealed a mortal sin in confession do more than repeat the sins</p> 
 <p>committed since his last worthy confession? </p> 
 <p>A. One who has willfully concealed a mortal sin in confession must, besides repeating all the sins he has committed since his last worthy confession, tell also how often he has unworthily received absolution and Holy Communion during the same time.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 800. Why does the priest give us a penance after Confession? </p> 
 <p>A. The priest gives us a penance after Confession, that we may satisfy God for the temporal punishment due to our sins.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 801. Why should we have to satisfy for our sins if Christ has fully satisfied for them? </p>  
 <p>A. Christ has fully satisfied for our sins and after our baptism we were free from all guilt and had no satisfaction to make. But when we willfully sinned after baptism, it is but just that we should be obliged to make some satisfaction.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 802. Is the slight penance the priest gives us sufficient to satisfy for all the sins confessed? </p> 
 <p>A. The slight penance the priest gives us is not sufficient to satisfy for all the sins confessed:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) Because there is no real equality between the slight penance given and the punishment deserved for sin; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) Because we are all obliged to do penance for sins committed, and this would not be necessary if the</p> 
 <p>     penance given in confession satisfied for all.</p>  
 <p>The penance is given and accepted in confession chiefly to show our willingness to do penance and make amends</p> 
 <p>for our sins.</p>  <b>
 </b><p>Q. 803. Does not the Sacrament of Penance remit all punishment due to sin? </p> 
 <p>A. The Sacrament of Penance remits the eternal punishment due to sin, but it does not always remit the temporal</p> 
 <p>punishment which God requires as satisfaction for our sins.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 804. Why does God require a temporal punishment as a satisfaction for sin? </p> 
 <p>A. God requires a temporal punishment as a satisfaction for sin to teach us the great evil of sin and to prevent us</p> 
 <p>from falling again.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 805. Which are the chief means by which we satisfy God for the temporal punishment due to sin? </p>  
 <p>A. The chief means by which we satisfy God for the temporal punishment due to sin are: Prayer, Fasting,</p> 
 <p>Almsgiving; all spiritual and corporal works of mercy, and the patient suffering of the ills of life.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 806. What fasting has the greatest merit? </p> 
 <p>A. The fasting imposed by the Church on certain days of the year, and particularly during Lent, has the greatest</p> 
 <p>merit.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 807. What is Lent? </p> 
 <p>A. Lent is the forty days before Easter Sunday, during which we do penance, fast and pray to prepare ourselves</p> 
 <p>for the resurrection of Our Lord; and also to remind us of His own fast of forty days before His Passion.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 808. What do we mean by "almsgiving"? </p> 
 <p>A. By almsgiving we mean money, goods, or assistance given to the poor or to charitable purposes. The law of</p> 
 <p>God requires all persons to give alms in proportion to their means.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 809. What "ills of life" help to satisfy God for sin? </p> 
 <p>A. The ills of life that help to satisfy God for sin are sickness, poverty, misfortune, trial, affliction, etc., especially,</p> 
 <p>when we have not brought them upon ourselves by sin.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 810. How did the Christians in the first ages of the Church do Penance? </p> 
 <p>A. The Christians in the first ages of the Church did public penance, especially for the sins of which they were</p> 
 <p>publicly known to be guilty. Penitents were excluded for a certain time from Mass or the Sacrament, and some</p> 
 <p>were obliged to stand at the door of the Church begging the prayers of those who entered.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 811. What were these severe Penances of the First Ages of the Church called? </p> 
 <p>A. These severe penances of the first ages of the Church were called canonical penances, because their kind and</p> 
 <p>duration were regulated by the Canons or laws of the Church.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 812. How can we know spiritual from corporal works of mercy? </p> 
 <p>A. We can know spiritual from corporal works of mercy, for whatever we do for the soul is a spiritual work, and</p> 
 <p>whatever we do for the body is a corporal work.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 813. Which are the chief spiritual works of mercy? </p> 
 <p>A. The chief spiritual works of mercy are seven:</p>  
 <p>   1.To admonish the sinner, to instruct the ignorant, to counsel the doubtful, to comfort the sorrowful, to bear</p> 
 <p>     wrongs patiently, to forgive all injuries, and to pray for the living and the dead.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 814. When are we bound to admonish the sinner? </p> 
 <p>A. We are bound to admonish the sinner when the following conditions are fulfilled:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) When his fault is a mortal sin; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) When we have authority or influence over him, and </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) When there is reason to believe that our warning will not make him worse instead of better.</p>  <b>
 </b><p>Q. 815. Who are meant by the "ignorant" we are to instruct, and the "doubtful" we are to counsel? </p> 
 <p>A. By the ignorant we are to instruct and the doubtful we are to counsel, are meant those particularly who are</p> 
 <p>ignorant of the truths of religion and those who are in doubt about matters of faith. We must aid such persons as far  as we can to know and believe the truths necessary for salvation.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 816. Why are we advised to bear wrong patiently and to forgive all injuries? </p> 
 <p>A. We are advised to bear wrongs patiently and to forgive all injuries, because, being Christians, we should imitate the example of Our Divine Lord, who endured wrongs patiently and who not only pardoned but prayed for those who injured Him.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 817. If, then, it be a Christian virtue to forgive all injuries, why do Christians establish courts and</p> 
 <p>prisons to punish wrongdoers? </p> 
 <p>A. Christians establish courts and prisons to punish wrongdoers, because the preservation of lawful authority, good order in society, the protection of others, and sometimes even the good of the guilty one himself, require that crimes be justly punished. As God Himself punishes crime and as lawful authority comes from Him, such authority has the right to punish, though individuals should forgive the injuries done to themselves personally.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 818. Why is it a work of mercy to pray for the living and the dead? </p> 
 <p>A. It is a work of mercy to aid those who are unable to aid themselves. The living are exposed to temptations, and</p> 
 <p>while in mortal sin they are deprived of the merit of their good works and need our prayers. The dead can in no</p> 
 <p>way help themselves and depend on us for assistance.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 819. Which are the chief corporal works of mercy? </p> 
 <p>A. The chief corporal works of mercy are seven:</p> 
 <p>   1.To feed the hungry, to give drink to the thirsty, to clothe the naked, to ransom the captive, to harbor the</p> 
 <p>     harborless, to visit the sick, and to bury the dead.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 820. How may we briefly state the corporal works of mercy? </p> 
 <p>A. We may briefly state the corporal works of mercy by saying that we are obliged to help the poor in all their</p> 
 <p>forms of want.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 821. How are Christians aided in the performance of works of mercy? </p> 
 <p>A. Christians are aided in the performance of works of mercy through the establishment of charitable institutions</p> 
 <p>where religious communities of holy men or women perform these duties for us, provided we supply the necessary means by our almsgiving and good works.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 822. Who are religious? </p> 
 <p>A. Religious are self-sacrificing men and women who, wishing to follow more closely the teachings of Our Lord,</p> 
 <p>dedicate their lives to the service of God and religion. They live together in societies approved by the Church,</p> 
 <p>under a rule and guidance of a superior. They keep the vows of chastity, poverty and obedience, and divide their</p> 
 <p>time between prayer and good works. The houses in which they dwell are called convents or monasteries, and the</p> 
 <p>societies in which they live are called religious orders, communities or congregations.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 823. Are there any religious communities of priests? </p> 
 <p>A. There are many religious communities of priests, who, besides living according to the general laws of the</p> 
 <p>Church, as all priests do, follow certain rules laid down for their community. Such priests are called the regular</p> 
 <p>clergy, because living by rules to distinguish them from the secular clergy who live in their parishes under no special rule. The chief work of the regular clergy is to teach in colleges and give missions and retreats.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 824. Why are there so many different religious communities? </p> 
 <p>A. There are many different religious communities:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) Because all religious are not fitted for the same work, and </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) Because they desire to imitate Our Lord's life on earth as perfectly as possible; and when each</p> 
 <p>     community takes one of Christ's works and seeks to become perfect in it, the union of all their works</p> 
 <p>     continues as perfectly as we can the works He began upon earth.</p>  
 <p> </p> 
 <p> </p> 
 <p>LESSON TWENTIETH: On the Manner of Making a Good Confession </p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 825. What should we do on entering the confessional? </p> 
 <p>A. On entering the confessional we should kneel, make the sign of the Cross, and say to the priest, "Bless me,</p> 
 <p>father"; then add, "I confess to Almighty God and to you, father, that I have sinned."</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 826. Which are the first things we should tell the priest in Confession? </p> 
 <p>A. The first things we should tell the priest in Confession are the time of our last Confession, and whether we said</p> 
 <p>the penance and went to Holy Communion.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 827. Should we tell anything else in connection with our last confession? </p> 
 <p>A. In connection with our last confession we should tell also what restrictions -- if any -- were placed upon us with  regard to our occasions of sin, and what obligations with regard to the payment of debts, restitution, injuries done to others and the like, we were commanded to fulfill.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 828. After telling the time of our last Confession and Communion what should we do? </p> 
 <p>A. After telling the time of our last Confession and Communion we should confess all the mortal sins we have since committed, and all the venial sins we may wish to mention.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 829. What is a general confession? </p> 
 <p>A. A general confession is the telling of the sins of our whole life or a great part of it. It is made in the same manner as an ordinary confession, except that it requires more time and longer preparation.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 830. When should a General Confession be made? </p> 
 <p>A. A general confession:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) Is necessary when we are certain that our past confessions were bad; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) It is useful on special occasions in our lives when some change in our way of living is about to take place;</p> 
 <p>   3.(3) It is hurtful and must not be made when persons are scrupulous.</p> <b> 
 </b><p> </p> 
 <p>Q. 831. What are the signs of scruples and the remedy against them? </p> 
 <p>A. The signs of scruples are chiefly:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) To be always dissatisfied with our confessions; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) To be self-willed in deciding what is sinful and what is not.</p>  
 <p>The chief remedy against them is to follow exactly the advice of the confessor without questioning the reason or</p> 
 <p>utility of his advice.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 832. What must we do when the confessor asks us questions? </p> 
 <p>A. When the confessor asks us questions we must answer them truthfully and clearly.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 833. What should we do after telling our sins? </p> 
 <p>A. After telling our sins we should listen with attention to the advice which the confessor may think proper to give.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 834. What duties does the priest perform in the confessional? </p> 
 <p>A. In the confessional the priest performs the duties:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) Of a judge, by listening to our self-accusations and passing sentence upon our guilt or innocence; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) Of a father, by the good advice and encouragement he gives us; </p> 
 <p>   3.(3) Of a teacher, by his instructions, and </p> 
 <p>   4.(4) Of a physician, by discovering the afflictions of our soul and giving us the remedies to restore it to</p> 
 <p>     spiritual health.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 835. Why is it beneficial to go always if possible to the same confessor? </p> 
 <p>A. It is beneficial to go always, if possible, to the same confessor, because our continued confessions enable him to see more clearly the true state of our soul and to understand better our occasions of sin.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 836. Should we remain away from confession because we cannot go to our usual confessor? </p> 
 <p>A. We should not remain away from confession because we cannot go to our usual confessor, for though it is well</p> 
 <p>to confess to the same priest, it is not necessary to do so. One should never become so attached to a confessor</p> 
 <p>that his absence or the great inconvenience of going to him would become an excuse for neglecting the Sacraments.</p>  <b>
 </b><p>Q. 837. How should we end our Confession? </p> 
 <p>A. We should end our Confession by saying, "I also accuse myself of all the sins of my past life," telling, if we</p> 
 <p>choose, one or several of our past sins.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 838. What should we do while the priest is giving us absolution? </p> 
 <p>A. While the priest is giving us absolution we should from our heart renew the Act of Contrition.</p>  
 <p> </p> 
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>LESSON TWENTY-FIRST: On Indulgences </p>  <b>
 </b><p>Q. 839. What is an Indulgence? </p> 
 <p>A. An Indulgence is the remission in whole or in part of the temporal punishment due to sin.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 840. What does the word "indulgence" mean? </p> 
 <p>A. The word indulgence means a favor or concession. An indulgence obtains by a very slight penance the</p> 
 <p>remission of penalties that would otherwise be severe.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 841. Is an Indulgence a pardon of sin, or a license to commit sin? </p> 
 <p>A. An Indulgence is not a pardon of sin, nor a license to commit sin, and one who is in a state of mortal sin cannot</p> 
 <p>gain an Indulgence.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 842. How do good works done in mortal sin profit us? </p> 
 <p>A. Good works done in mortal sin profit us by obtaining for us the grace to repent and sometimes temporal</p> 
 <p>blessings. Mortal sin deprives us of all our merit, nevertheless God will bestow gifts for every good deed as He will punish every evil deed.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 843. How many kinds of Indulgences are there? </p> 
 <p>A. There are two kinds of Indulgences -- Plenary and Partial.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 844. What is Plenary Indulgence? </p> 
 <p>A. A Plenary Indulgence is the full remission of the temporal punishment due to sin.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 845. Is it easy to gain a Plenary Indulgence? </p> 
 <p>A. It is not easy to gain a Plenary Indulgence, as we may understand from its great privilege. To gain a Plenary</p> 
 <p>Indulgence, we must hate sin, be heartily sorry for even our venial sins, and have no desire for even the slightest sin.  Though we may not gain entirely each Plenary Indulgence we seek, we always gain a part of each; that is, a partial indulgence, greater or less in proportion to our good dispositions.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 846. Which are the most important Plenary Indulgences granted by the Church? </p> 
 <p>A. The most important Plenary Indulgences granted by the Church are:</p>  
 <p>   1.(1) The Indulgences of a jubilee which the Pope grants every twenty-five years or on great occasions by</p> 
 <p>     which he gives special faculties to confessors for the absolution of reserved sins; </p> 
 <p>   2.(2) The Indulgence granted to the dying in their last agony.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 847. What is a Partial Indulgence? </p> 
 <p>A. A Partial Indulgence is the remission of part of the temporal punishment due to sin.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 848. How long has the practice of granting Indulgences been in use in the Church, and what was its</p> 
 <p>origin? </p> 
 <p>A. The practice of granting Indulgences has been in use in the Church since the time of the apostles. It had its origin  in the earnest prayers of holy persons, and especially of the martyrs begging the Church for their sake to shorten the severe penances of sinners, or to change them into lighter penances. The request was frequently granted and the penance remitted, shortened or changed, and with the penance remitted the temporal punishment</p> 
 <p>corresponding to it was blotted out.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 849. How do we show that the Church has the power to grant Indulgences? </p> 
 <p>A. We show that the Church has the power to grant Indulgences, because Christ has given it power to remit all</p> 
 <p>guilt without restriction, and if the Church has power, in the Sacrament of penance, to remit the eternal punishment -- which is the greatest -- it must have power to remit the temporal or lesser punishment, even outside the Sacrament of Penance.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 850. How do we know that these Indulgences have their effect? </p> 
 <p>A. We know that these Indulgences have their effect, because the Church, through her councils, declares</p> 
 <p>Indulgences useful, and if they have no effect they would be useless, and the Church would teach error in spite of</p> 
 <p>Christ's promise to guide it.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 851. Have there ever existed abuses among the faithful in the manner of using Indulgences? </p> 
 <p>A. There have existed, in past ages, some abuses among the faithful in the manner of using Indulgences, and the</p> 
 <p>Church has always labored to correct such abuses as soon as possible. In the use of pious practices we must be</p> 
 <p>always guided by our lawful superiors.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 852. How have the enemies of the Church made use of the abuse of Indulgences? </p> 
 <p>A. The enemies of the Church have made use of the abuse of Indulgences to deny the doctrine of Indulgences, and to break down the teaching and limit the power of the Church. Not to be deceived in matters of faith, we must</p> 
 <p>always distinguish very carefully between the abuses to which a devotion may lead and the truths upon which the</p> 
 <p>devotion rests.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 853. How does the Church by means of Indulgences remit the temporal punishment due to sin? </p> 
 <p>A. The Church, by means of Indulgences, remits the temporal punishment due to sin by applying to us the merits of Jesus Christ, and the superabundant satisfactions of the Blessed Virgin Mary and of the saints; which merits and satisfactions are its spiritual treasury.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 854. What do we mean by the "superabundant satisfaction of the Blessed Virgin and the Saints"? </p> 
 <p>A. By the superabundant satisfaction of the Blessed Virgin and the saints, we mean all the satisfaction over and</p> 
 <p>above what was necessary to satisfy for their own sins. As their good works were many and their sins few -- the</p> 
 <p>Blessed Virgin being sinless -- the satisfaction not needed for themselves is kept by the Church in a spiritual</p> 
 <p>treasury to be used for our benefit.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 855. Does the Church, by granting Indulgences, free us from doing Penance? </p> 
 <p>A. The Church, by granting Indulgences, does not free us from doing penance, but simply makes our penance</p> 
 <p>lighter that we may more easily satisfy for our sins and escape the punishments they deserve.</p> <b> 
 </b><p> </p> 
 <p>Q. 856. Who has the power to grant Indulgences? </p> 
 <p>A. The Pope alone has the power to grant Indulgences for the whole Church; but the bishops have power to grant</p> 
 <p>partial Indulgences in their own diocese. Cardinals and some others, by the special permission of the Pope, have</p> 
 <p>the right to grant certain Indulgences.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 857. Where shall we find the Indulgences granted by the Church? </p> 
 <p>A. We shall find the Indulgences granted by the Church in the declarations of the Pope and of the Sacred</p> 
 <p>Congregation of Cardinals. These declarations are usually put into prayer books and books of devotion or</p> 
 <p>instruction.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 858. What must we do to gain an Indulgence? </p> 
 <p>A. To gain an Indulgence we must be in the state of grace and perform the works enjoined.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 859. Besides being in a state of grace and performing the works enjoined, what else is necessary for</p> 
 <p>the gaining of an Indulgence? </p> 
 <p>A. Besides being in a state of grace and performing the works enjoined, it is necessary for the gaining of an</p> 
 <p>Indulgence to have at least the general intention of gaining it.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 860. How and why should we make a general intention to gain all possible Indulgences each day? </p> 
 <p>A. We should make a general intention at our morning prayers to gain all possible Indulgences each day, because</p> 
 <p>several of the prayers we say and good works we perform may have Indulgences attached to them, though we are</p> 
 <p>not aware of it.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 861. What works are generally enjoined for the gaining of Indulgences? </p> 
 <p>A. The works generally enjoined for the gaining of Indulgences are: The saying of certain prayers, fasting, and the use of certain articles of devotion; visits to Churches or altars, and the giving of alms. For the gaining of Plenary Indulgences it is generally required to go to confession and Holy Communion and pray for the intention of the Pope.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 862. What does praying for a person's intention mean? </p> 
 <p>A. Praying for a person's intention means praying for whatever he prays for or desires to obtain through prayer --</p> 
 <p>some spiritual or temporal favors.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 863. What does an Indulgence of forty days mean? </p> 
 <p>A. An Indulgence of forty days means that for the prayer or work to which an Indulgence of forty days is attached, God remits as much of our temporal punishment as He remitted for forty days' canonical penance. We do not know just how much temporal punishment God remitted for forty days' public penance, but whatever it was, He remits the same now when we gain an Indulgence of forty days. The same rule applies to Indulgences of a year or any length of time.</p>  
 <p> </p> <b>
 </b><p>Q. 864. Why did the Church moderate its severe penances? </p> 
 <p>A. The Church moderated its severe penances, because when Christians -- terrified by persecution -- grew</p>
